width:14.772ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>So for the gravitational force &#8211; or, more generally, for <i>any</i> inverse square force law &#8211; the right hand side of the equation becomes a constant and the equation is seen to be the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator" title="Harmonic oscillator">harmonic equation</a> (up to a shift of origin of the dependent variable). The solution is:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle u(\\theta )={\\frac {\\mu }{h^{2}}}-A\\cos(\\theta -\\theta _{0})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>u</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mi>cos</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle u(\\theta )={\\frac {\\mu }{h^{2}}}-A\\cos(\\theta -\\theta _{0})}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle u(\\theta )={\\frac {\\mu }{h^{2}}}-A\\cos(\\theta -\\theta _{0})}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f53bda05eeacf1a39da99dc5b738b9f909dbd2a1" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:26.524ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>A</i> and <i>&#952;</i><sub>0</sub> are arbitrary constants.\nThis resulting equation of the orbit of the object is that of an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse#Polar_form_relative_to_focus" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> in Polar form relative to one of the focal points. This is put into a more standard form by\nletting <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle e\\equiv h^{2}A/\\mu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle e\\equiv h^{2}A/\\mu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="e\\equiv h^{2}A/\\mu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1140979616948d79c62f66180b7f249f77dcaeff" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.882ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(orbit)" title="Eccentricity (orbit)">eccentricity</a>,\nletting <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle a\\equiv h^{2}/(\\mu (1-e^{2}))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle a\\equiv h^{2}/(\\mu (1-e^{2}))}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="a\\equiv h^{2}/(\\mu (1-e^{2}))" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a9ffc4a0617a2cbc866796245ab3603ad0b34757" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.045ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> be the semi-major axis.\nFinally, letting <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\theta _{0}\\equiv 0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8801;<!-- &#8801; --></mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\theta _{0}\\equiv 0}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\theta _{0}\\equiv 0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/272d6afef5d598d137d6e645b78f88e9ce5d92fe" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.406ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> so the long axis of the ellipse is along the positive <i>x</i> coordinate.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r(\\theta )={\\frac {a(1-e^{2})}{1-e\\cos \\theta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>cos</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r(\\theta )={\\frac {a(1-e^{2})}{1-e\\cos \\theta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle r(\\theta )={\\frac {a(1-e^{2})}{1-e\\cos \\theta }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7ca99893bcd4beb2e7df00ea4ac6996b88f0bb5" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:17.945ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativistic_orbital_motion">Relativistic orbital motion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Relativistic orbital motion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The above classical (<a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">Newtonian</a>) analysis of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_mechanics" title="Orbital mechanics">orbital mechanics</a> assumes that the more subtle effects of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frame_dragging" title="Frame dragging">frame dragging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_time_dilation" title="Gravitational time dilation">gravitational time dilation</a> are negligible. Relativistic effects cease to be negligible when near very massive bodies (as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler_problem_in_general_relativity" title="Kepler problem in general relativity">precession of Mercury\'s orbit</a> about the Sun), or when extreme precision is needed (as with calculations of the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a> and time signal references for <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System#Relativity" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>)\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_planes">Orbital planes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Orbital planes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">Orbital plane (astronomy)</a></div>\n<p>The analysis so far has been two dimensional; it turns out that an <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory" title="Perturbation theory">unperturbed</a> orbit is two-dimensional in a plane fixed in space, and thus the extension to three dimensions requires simply rotating the two-dimensional plane into the required angle relative to the poles of the planetary body involved.\n</p><p>The rotation to do this in three dimensions requires three numbers to uniquely determine; traditionally these are expressed as three angles.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_period">Orbital period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orbital period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">Orbital period</a></div>\n<p>The orbital period is simply how long an orbiting body takes to complete one orbit.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifying_orbits">Specifying orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Specifying orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_trajectory" title="Orbital trajectory">Orbital trajectory</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keplerian_elements" title="Keplerian elements">Keplerian elements</a></div>\n<p>Six parameters are required to specify a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keplerian_orbit" title="Keplerian orbit">Keplerian orbit</a> about a body. For example, the three numbers that specify the body\'s initial position, and the three values that specify its velocity will define a unique orbit that can be calculated forwards (or backwards) in time. However, traditionally the parameters used are slightly different.\n</p><p>The traditionally used set of orbital elements is called the set of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">Keplerian elements</a>, after Johannes Kepler and his laws. The Keplerian elements are six:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">Inclination</a> (<i>i</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Longitude_of_the_ascending_node" title="Longitude of the ascending node">Longitude of the ascending node</a> (&#937;)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Argument_of_periapsis" title="Argument of periapsis">Argument of periapsis</a> (&#969;)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">Eccentricity</a> (<i>e</i>)</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semimajor_axis" title="Semimajor axis">Semimajor axis</a> (<i>a</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mean_anomaly" title="Mean anomaly">Mean anomaly</a> at <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">epoch</a> (<i>M</i><sub>0</sub>).</li></ul>\n<p>In principle once the orbital elements are known for a body, its position can be calculated forward and backwards indefinitely in time. However, in practice, orbits are affected or <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbed</a>, by other forces than simple gravity from an assumed point source (see the next section), and thus the orbital elements change over time.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_perturbations">Orbital perturbations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orbital perturbations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>An orbital perturbation is when a force or impulse which is much smaller than the overall force or average impulse of the main gravitating body and which is external to the two orbiting bodies causes an acceleration, which changes the parameters of the orbit over time.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Radial.2C_prograde_and_transverse_perturbations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Radial,_prograde_and_transverse_perturbations">Radial, prograde and transverse perturbations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radial, prograde and transverse perturbations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A small radial impulse given to a body in orbit changes the <a href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(mathematics)" title="Eccentricity (mathematics)">eccentricity</a>, but not the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> (to first order). A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">prograde</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde</a> impulse (i.e. an impulse applied along the orbital motion) changes both the eccentricity and the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a>. Notably, a prograde impulse at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a> raises the altitude at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoapsis" title="Apoapsis">apoapsis</a>, and vice versa, and a retrograde impulse does the opposite. A transverse impulse (out of the orbital plane) causes rotation of the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">orbital plane</a> without changing the <a href="/wiki/Orbit_(dynamics)" title="Orbit (dynamics)">period</a> or eccentricity. In all instances, a closed orbit will still intersect the perturbation point.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_decay">Orbital decay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Orbital decay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_decay" title="Orbital decay">Orbital decay</a></div>\n<p>If an orbit is about a planetary body with significant atmosphere, its orbit can decay because of <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>. Particularly at each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a>, the object experiences atmospheric drag, losing energy. Each time, the orbit grows less eccentric (more circular) because the object loses kinetic energy precisely when that energy is at its maximum. This is similar to the effect of slowing a pendulum at its lowest point; the highest point of the pendulum\'s swing becomes lower. With each successive slowing more of the orbit\'s path is affected by the atmosphere and the effect becomes more pronounced. Eventually, the effect becomes so great that the maximum kinetic energy is not enough to return the orbit above the limits of the atmospheric drag effect. When this happens the body will rapidly spiral down and intersect the central body.\n</p><p>The bounds of an atmosphere vary wildly. During a <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">solar maximum</a>, the Earth\'s atmosphere causes drag up to a hundred kilometres higher than during a solar minimum.\n</p><p>Some satellites with long conductive tethers can also experience orbital decay because of electromagnetic drag from the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a>. As the wire cuts the magnetic field it acts as a generator, moving electrons from one end to the other. The orbital energy is converted to heat in the wire.\n</p><p>Orbits can be artificially influenced through the use of rocket engines which change the kinetic energy of the body at some point in its path. This is the conversion of chemical or electrical energy to kinetic energy. In this way changes in the orbit shape or orientation can be facilitated.\n</p><p>Another method of artificially influencing an orbit is through the use of <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">solar sails</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_sail" title="Magnetic sail">magnetic sails</a>. These forms of propulsion require no propellant or energy input other than that of the Sun, and so can be used indefinitely. See <a href="/wiki/Statite" title="Statite">statite</a> for one such proposed use.\n</p><p>Orbital decay can occur due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> for objects below the <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">synchronous orbit</a> for the body they\'re orbiting. The gravity of the orbiting object raises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_bulge" title="Tidal bulge">tidal bulges</a> in the primary, and since below the synchronous orbit the orbiting object is moving faster than the body\'s surface the bulges lag a short angle behind it. The gravity of the bulges is slightly off of the primary-satellite axis and thus has a component along the satellite\'s motion. The near bulge slows the object more than the far bulge speeds it up, and as a result the orbit decays. Conversely, the gravity of the satellite on the bulges applies <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> on the primary and speeds up its rotation. Artificial satellites are too small to have an appreciable tidal effect on the planets they orbit, but several moons in the Solar System are undergoing orbital decay by this mechanism. Mars\' innermost moon <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> is a prime example, and is expected to either impact Mars\' surface or break up into a ring within 50 million years.\n</p><p>Orbits can decay via the emission of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>. This mechanism is extremely weak for most stellar objects, only becoming significant in cases where there is a combination of extreme mass and extreme acceleration, such as with <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a> that are orbiting each other closely.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oblateness">Oblateness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Oblateness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The standard analysis of orbiting bodies assumes that all bodies consist of uniform spheres, or more generally, concentric shells each of uniform density. It can be shown that such bodies are gravitationally equivalent to point sources.\n</p><p>However, in the real world, many bodies rotate, and this introduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblateness" title="Oblateness">oblateness</a> and distorts the gravity field, and gives a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadropole#Gravitational_quadrupole" title="Quadropole">quadrupole</a> moment to the gravitational field which is significant at distances comparable to the radius of the body. In the general case, the gravitational potential of a rotating body such as, e.g., a planet is usually expanded in multipoles accounting for the departures of it from spherical symmetry. From the point of view of satellite dynamics, of particular relevance are the so-called even zonal harmonic coefficients, or even zonals, since they induce secular orbital perturbations which are cumulative over time spans longer than the orbital period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> They do depend on the orientation of the body\'s symmetry axis in the space, affecting, in general, the whole orbit, with the exception of the semimajor axis.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_gravitating_bodies">Multiple gravitating bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Multiple gravitating bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/N-body_problem" title="N-body problem">n-body problem</a></div>\n<p>The effects of other gravitating bodies can be significant. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_the_Moon" title="Orbit of the Moon">orbit of the Moon</a> cannot be accurately described without allowing for the action of the Sun\'s gravity as well as the Earth\'s. One approximate result is that bodies will usually have reasonably stable orbits around a heavier planet or moon, in spite of these perturbations, provided they are orbiting well within the heavier body\'s <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a>.\n</p><p>When there are more than two gravitating bodies it is referred to as an <a href="/wiki/N-body_problem" title="N-body problem">n-body problem</a>. Most n-body problems have no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_form_solution" title="Closed form solution">closed form solution</a>, although some special cases have been formulated.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_radiation_and_stellar_wind">Light radiation and stellar wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Light radiation and stellar wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For smaller bodies particularly, light and <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar wind</a> can cause significant perturbations to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_(geometry)" title="Attitude (geometry)">attitude</a> and direction of motion of the body, and over time can be significant. Of the planetary bodies, the motion of <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> is particularly affected over large periods when the asteroids are rotating relative to the Sun.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Strange_orbits">Strange orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Strange orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Mathematicians have discovered that it is possible in principle to have multiple bodies in non-elliptical orbits that repeat periodically, although most such orbits are not stable regarding small perturbations in mass, position, or velocity. However, some special stable cases have been identified, including a planar figure-eight orbit occupied by <a href="/wiki/Three-body_problem" title="Three-body problem">three moving bodies</a>. Further studies have discovered that nonplanar orbits are also possible, including one involving 12 masses moving in 4 roughly circular, interlocking orbits <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topologically</a> equivalent to the edges of a <a href="/wiki/Cuboctahedron" title="Cuboctahedron">cuboctahedron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Finding such orbits naturally occurring in the universe is thought to be extremely unlikely, because of the improbability of the required conditions occurring by chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astrodynamics">Astrodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Astrodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_mechanics" title="Orbital mechanics">Orbital mechanics</a></div>\n<p><b>Orbital mechanics</b> or <b>astrodynamics</b> is the application of <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">ballistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial mechanics</a> to the practical problems concerning the motion of <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. The motion of these objects is usually calculated from <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton\'s laws of motion">Newton\'s laws of motion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation">Newton\'s law of universal gravitation</a>. It is a core discipline within space mission design and control. Celestial mechanics treats more broadly the orbital dynamics of systems under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, including spacecraft and natural astronomical bodies such as star systems, <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>. Orbital mechanics focuses on spacecraft <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectories</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Orbital_maneuver" title="Orbital maneuver">orbital maneuvers</a>, orbit plane changes, and interplanetary transfers, and is used by mission planners to predict the results of <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsive maneuvers</a>. <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">General relativity</a> is a more exact theory than Newton\'s laws for calculating orbits, and is sometimes necessary for greater accuracy or in high-gravity situations (such as orbits close to the Sun).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_orbits">Earth orbits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Earth orbits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:251px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg/249px-Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg/374px-Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg/498px-Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg.png 2x" width="249"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_satellite_navigation_orbits.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of geostationary Earth orbit with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a>, <a href="/wiki/GLONASS" title="GLONASS">GLONASS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(satellite_navigation)" title="Galileo (satellite navigation)">Galileo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_navigation_system" title="Compass navigation system">Compass (medium Earth orbit)</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation_system" title="Satellite navigation system">satellite navigation system</a> orbits with the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iridium_constellation" title="Iridium constellation">Iridium constellation</a> orbits, and the nominal size of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[a]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>\'s orbit is around 9 times larger (in radius and length) than geostationary orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[b]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_orbits" title="List of orbits">List of orbits</a></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">Low Earth orbit</a> (LEO): <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Geocentric orbits</a> with altitudes up to 2,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km" title="Km">km</a> (0&#8211;1,240 <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">miles</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Medium_Earth_orbit" title="Medium Earth orbit">Medium Earth orbit</a> (MEO): <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Geocentric orbits</a> ranging in altitude from 2,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km" title="Km">km</a> (1,240 <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">miles</a>) to just below <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbit</a> at 35,786 kilometers (22,236&#160;mi).  Also known as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermediate_circular_orbit" title="Intermediate circular orbit">intermediate circular orbit</a>.  These are "most commonly at 20,200 kilometers (12,600&#160;mi), or 20,650 kilometers (12,830&#160;mi), with an orbital period of 12 hours."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_orbit_definition_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_orbit_definition-17">[15]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Both <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbit</a> (GSO) and <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a> (GEO) are orbits around Earth matching Earth\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_rotation" title="Sidereal rotation">sidereal rotation</a> period.  All geosynchronous and geostationary orbits have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> of 42,164&#160;km (26,199&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> All geostationary orbits are also geosynchronous, but not all geosynchronous orbits are geostationary. A geostationary orbit stays exactly above the equator, whereas a geosynchronous orbit may swing north and south to cover more of the Earth\'s surface. Both complete one full orbit of Earth per sidereal day (relative to the stars, not the Sun).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/High_Earth_orbit" title="High Earth orbit">High Earth orbit</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Geocentric orbits</a> above the altitude of <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbit</a> 35,786 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km" title="Km">km</a> (22,240 <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">miles</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_orbit_definition_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_orbit_definition-17">[15]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scaling_in_gravity">Scaling in gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Scaling in gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> <i>G</i> has been calculated as:\n</p>\n<ul><li>(6.6742 &#177; 0.001) &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;11</sup> (kg/m<sup>3</sup>)<sup>&#8722;1</sup>s<sup>&#8722;2</sup>.</li></ul>\n<p>Thus the constant has dimension density<sup>&#8722;1</sup> time<sup>&#8722;2</sup>. This corresponds to the following properties.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Scale_factor" title="Scale factor">Scaling</a> of distances (including sizes of bodies, while keeping the densities the same) gives <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similar</a> orbits without scaling the time: if for example distances are halved, masses are divided by 8, gravitational forces by 16 and gravitational accelerations by 2. Hence velocities are halved and orbital periods remain the same. Similarly, when an object is dropped from a tower, the time it takes to fall to the ground remains the same with a scale model of the tower on a scale model of the Earth.\n</p><p>Scaling of distances while keeping the masses the same (in the case of point masses, or by reducing the densities) gives similar orbits; if distances are multiplied by 4, gravitational forces and accelerations are divided by 16, velocities are halved and orbital periods are multiplied by 8.\n</p><p>When all densities are multiplied by 4, orbits are the same; gravitational forces are multiplied by 16 and accelerations by 4, velocities are doubled and orbital periods are halved.\n</p><p>When all densities are multiplied by 4, and all sizes are halved, orbits are similar; masses are divided by 2, gravitational forces are the same, gravitational accelerations are doubled. Hence velocities are the same and orbital periods are halved.\n</p><p>In all these cases of scaling. if densities are multiplied by 4, times are halved; if velocities are doubled, forces are multiplied by 16.\n</p><p>These properties are illustrated in the formula (derived from the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period#Small_body_orbiting_a_central_body" title="Orbital period">formula for the orbital period</a>)\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle GT^{2}\\sigma =3\\pi \\left({\\frac {a}{r}}\\right)^{3},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>G</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>3</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mi>r</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle GT^{2}\\sigma =3\\pi \\left({\\frac {a}{r}}\\right)^{3},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="GT^{2}\\sigma =3\\pi \\left({\\frac  {a}{r}}\\right)^{3}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/92535af69773d85afeb2cc17e4c2c9267350d02d" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:18.066ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>for an elliptical orbit with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> <i>a</i>, of a small body around a spherical body with radius <i>r</i> and average density &#963;, where <i>T</i> is the orbital period. See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Third_Law" title="Kepler\'s Third Law">Kepler\'s Third Law</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patents">Patents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Patents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The application of certain orbits or orbital maneuvers to specific useful purposes have been the subject of patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_locking">Tidal locking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Tidal locking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">Tidal locking</a></div>\n<p>Some bodies are tidally locked with other bodies, meaning that one side of the celestial body is permanently facing its host object. This is the case for Sun-<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, Earth-<a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and Pluto-Charon system.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li title="Ephemeris">Ephemeris</a> is a compilation of positions of naturally occurring astronomical objects as well as artificial satellites in the sky at a given time or times.</li>\n<li><a title="Rosetta (orbit)">Rosetta (orbit)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/VSOP_(planets)" title="VSOP (planets)">VSOP (planets)</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Orbit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Orbital periods and speeds are calculated using the relations 4<span km).</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
